<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Syd Barrett</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">

<p><b>Roger Keith</b> "<b>Syd</b>" <b>Barrett</b> (6 January 1946 – 7 July 2006) was an English musician, composer, singer-songwriter, and painter. Best known as a founder member of the band <a href="/wiki/Pink_Floyd" title="Pink Floyd">Pink Floyd</a>, Barrett was the lead singer, guitarist and principal songwriter in its early years and is credited with naming the band. Barrett was excluded from Pink Floyd in April 1968 after <a href="/wiki/David_Gilmour" title="David Gilmour">David Gilmour</a> took over as their new guitarist, and was briefly hospitalised amid speculation of <a href="/wiki/Mental_disorder" title="Mental disorder">mental illness</a>.</p>
<p>Barrett was musically active for less than ten years. With Pink Floyd, he recorded four singles, their <a href="/wiki/The_Piper_at_the_Gates_of_Dawn" title="The Piper at the Gates of Dawn">debut album</a> (and contributed to <a href="/wiki/A_Saucerful_of_Secrets" title="A Saucerful of Secrets">the second one</a>), and several unreleased songs. Barrett began his solo career in 1969 with the single "<a href="/wiki/Octopus_(Syd_Barrett_song)" title="Octopus (Syd Barrett song)">Octopus</a>" from his first solo album, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Madcap_Laughs" title="The Madcap Laughs">The Madcap Laughs</a></i> (1970). The album was recorded over the course of a year with five different producers (<a href="/wiki/Peter_Jenner" title="Peter Jenner">Peter Jenner</a>, Malcolm Jones, <a href="/wiki/David_Gilmour" title="David Gilmour">David Gilmour</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roger_Waters" title="Roger Waters">Roger Waters</a> and Barrett himself). Nearly two months after <i>Madcap</i> was released, Barrett began working on his second and final album, <i><a href="/wiki/Barrett_(album)" title="Barrett (album)">Barrett</a></i> (1970), produced by Gilmour and featuring contributions from <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wright_(musician)" title="Richard Wright (musician)">Richard Wright</a>. Two years later, he left the music industry, retired from public life and strictly guarded his own privacy until his death in 2006. In 1988, an album of unreleased tracks and outtakes, <i><a href="/wiki/Opel_(album)" title="Opel (album)">Opel</a></i>, was released by <a href="/wiki/EMI" title="EMI">EMI</a> with Barrett's approval.</p>
<p>Barrett's innovative guitar work and exploration of experimental techniques such as <a href="/wiki/Consonance_and_dissonance" title="Consonance and dissonance">dissonance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Distortion" title="Distortion">distortion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Feedback" title="Feedback">feedback</a> influenced many musicians. His recordings are also noted for their strongly English-accented vocal delivery. After leaving music, Barrett continued with painting and dedicated himself to gardening. Pink Floyd wrote and recorded several tributes to him, most notably the 1975 album <i><a href="/wiki/Wish_You_Were_Here_(Pink_Floyd_album)" title="Wish You Were Here (Pink Floyd album)">Wish You Were Here</a></i>, which included "<a href="/wiki/Shine_On_You_Crazy_Diamond" title="Shine On You Crazy Diamond">Shine On You Crazy Diamond</a>", as homage to Barrett.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biography">Biography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Syd_Barrett&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Biography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_years">Early years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Syd_Barrett&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Syd Barrett was born as Roger Keith Barrett in the English city of <a href="/wiki/Cambridge" title="Cambridge">Cambridge</a> to a middle-class family living at 60 Glisson Road.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning3_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning3-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irghd3-4_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irghd3-4-4">[4]</a></sup> Barrett was the fourth of five children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-5">[5]</a></sup> His father, <a href="/wiki/Max_Barrett" title="Max Barrett">Arthur Max Barrett</a>, was a prominent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pathologist" title="Pathologist">pathologist</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning3_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning3-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blake13_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-blake13-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irghd4_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irghd4-7">[7]</a></sup> and he was related to <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Garrett_Anderson" title="Elizabeth Garrett Anderson">Elizabeth Garrett Anderson</a>, through Max's maternal grandmother Ellen Garrett, who was Elizabeth's cousin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blake13_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-blake13-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irghd4_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Irghd4-7">[7]</a></sup> In 1951 his family moved to 183 <a href="/wiki/Hills_Road,_Cambridge" title="Hills Road, Cambridge">Hills Road</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning3_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-manning3-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irghd3-4_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Irghd3-4-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Barrett played piano occasionally, but usually preferred writing and drawing. He got a ukulele at 10, a banjo at 11<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning20069.E2.80.9310_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning20069.E2.80.9310-8">[8]</a></sup> and a Hofner <a href="/wiki/Acoustic_guitar" title="Acoustic guitar">acoustic guitar</a> at 14.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pal001_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-pal001-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning200610_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning200610-10">[10]</a></sup> A year after he got his first acoustic guitar, he bought his first electric guitar and built his own <a href="/wiki/Amplifier" title="Amplifier">amplifier</a>. One story of how Barrett acquired the nickname "Syd" is that at the age of 14 he was named after an old local Cambridge <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a> double bassist,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning200610_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning200610-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChapman201012_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChapman201012-11">[11]</a></sup> Sid "The Beat" Barrett, which claims Syd Barrett changed the spelling to differentiate himself from his namesake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mas001_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-mas001-12">[12]</a></sup> Another story is that when he was 13, his schoolmates nicknamed him "Syd" after he showed up to a field day at Abington Scout site wearing a <a href="/wiki/Flat_cap" title="Flat cap">flat cap</a> instead of his <a href="/wiki/Boy_Scouts" title="Boy Scouts">Scout</a> beret because "Syd" was a "working-class" name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChapman201011.E2.80.9312_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChapman201011.E2.80.9312-13">[13]</a></sup> He used both names interchangeably for several years. His sister Rosemary stated, "He was never Syd at home. He would never have allowed it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChapman201012_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChapman201012-11">[11]</a></sup> He was a Scout with the 7th Cambridge troop and went on to be a patrol leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>At one point at Morley Memorial Junior School he was taught by <a href="/wiki/Roger_Waters" title="Roger Waters">Roger Waters</a>' mother, Mary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChapman20108_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChapman20108-15">[15]</a></sup> Later, in 1957, he attended <a href="/wiki/Cambridgeshire_High_School_for_Boys" title="Cambridgeshire High School for Boys">Cambridgeshire High School for Boys</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChapman20109_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChapman20109-16">[16]</a></sup> (with Waters).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning3_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-manning3-3">[3]</a></sup> His father died of cancer on 11 December 1961,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning200610_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning200610-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlake200817_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlake200817-17">[17]</a></sup> less than a month before Barrett's 16th birthday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChapman201031_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChapman201031-18">[18]</a></sup> Also on this day, Barrett had left the entry in his diary for this date blank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning200610_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning200610-10">[10]</a></sup> By this time, his brothers and sisters had left home and his mother decided to rent out rooms to lodgers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlake200817_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlake200817-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChapman201033_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChapman201033-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cambridge-news.co.uk_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-cambridge-news.co.uk-20">[20]</a></sup> Eager to help her son recover from his grief, Barrett's mother encouraged the band in which he played, Geoff Mott and The Mottoes, a band which Barrett formed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning200610_10-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning200610-10">[10]</a></sup> to perform in their front room. Roger Waters and Syd Barrett were childhood friends, and Waters often visited such gigs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning3_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-manning3-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning200610_10-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning200610-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchaffner200522-23_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaffner200522-23-21">[21]</a></sup> At one point, Waters even organised a gig, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CND" title="CND">CND</a> benefit at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Friends_Meeting_House" title="Friends Meeting House">Friends Meeting House</a> on 11 March 1962,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning3_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-manning3-3">[3]</a></sup> but shortly afterwards Geoff Mott joined the Boston Crabs, and the Mottoes broke up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning200610_10-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning200610-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>In September 1962, Barrett had taken a place at the Cambridge Technical College art department,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irghd40_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irghd40-22">[22]</a></sup> where he met <a href="/wiki/David_Gilmour" title="David Gilmour">David Gilmour</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning5_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning5-23">[23]</a></sup> During the winter of 1962 and early 1963, the <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">Beatles</a> made an impact on Barrett, and he began to play Beatles songs at parties and at picnics. In 1963, Barrett became a <a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones" title="The Rolling Stones">Rolling Stones</a> fan and Barrett and then-girlfriend Libby Gausden saw them perform at a village hall in Cambridgeshire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning5_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning5-23">[23]</a></sup> It was at this point Barrett started writing songs; one friend recalls hearing "Effervescing Elephant" (later to be recorded on his solo album <i><a href="/wiki/Barrett_(album)" title="Barrett (album)">Barrett</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning200611.E2.80.9312_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning200611.E2.80.9312-24">[24]</a></sup> Also around this time, Barrett and Gilmour occasionally played acoustic gigs together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning6_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning6-25">[25]</a></sup> Barrett had played bass guitar with Those Without during the summer of 1963<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning6_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning6-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh50_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh50-26">[26]</a></sup> and both bass and guitar with The Hollerin' Blues the next summer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning6_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-manning6-25">[25]</a></sup> In 1964, Barrett and Gausden saw <a href="/wiki/Bob_Dylan" title="Bob Dylan">Bob Dylan</a> perform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning5_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-manning5-23">[23]</a></sup> After this performance, Barrett was inspired to write "<a href="/wiki/Bob_Dylan_Blues" title="Bob Dylan Blues">Bob Dylan Blues</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh58_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh58-27">[27]</a></sup> Barrett, now thinking about his future,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning6_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-manning6-25">[25]</a></sup> decided to apply for <a href="/wiki/Camberwell_College_of_Arts" title="Camberwell College of Arts">Camberwell College of Arts</a> in London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irghd45_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irghd45-28">[28]</a></sup> Barrett enrolled in the college in the summer of 1964<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning6_25-4"><a href="#cite_note-manning6-25">[25]</a></sup> to study painting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Times_obit_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Times_obit-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pink_Floyd_years_.281965.E2.80.9368.29">Pink Floyd years (1965–68)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Syd_Barrett&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Pink Floyd years (1965–68)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pink_Floyd#1963.E2.80.9367:_Early_years" title="Pink Floyd">Pink Floyd § 1963–67: Early years</a></div>
<p>Starting in 1964, the band that would become Pink Floyd evolved through various line-up and name changes including "The Abdabs",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning200614_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning200614-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh52_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh52-31">[31]</a></sup> "The Screaming Abdabs",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh52_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh52-31">[31]</a></sup> "Sigma 6",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh52_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh52-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlake200838_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlake200838-32">[32]</a></sup> and "The Meggadeaths".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh52_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh52-31">[31]</a></sup> In 1965, Barrett joined them as The Tea Set<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh52_31-4"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh52-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning9_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning9-33">[33]</a></sup> (sometimes spelled T-Set).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blake43_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-blake43-34">[34]</a></sup> When they found themselves playing a concert with another band of the same name, Barrett came up with "The Pink Floyd Sound" (also known as "The Pink Floyd Blues Band",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-blake43_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-blake43-34">[34]</a></sup> later "The Pink Floyd").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[nb 1]</a></sup> During 1965, they went into a studio for the first time, when a friend of <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wright_(musician)" title="Richard Wright (musician)">Richard Wright</a>'s gave the band free time to record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[nb 2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning9_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning9-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>During this summer Barrett had his first <a href="/wiki/Lysergic_acid_diethylamide" title="Lysergic acid diethylamide">LSD</a> trip in the garden of friend Dave Gale,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning7_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning7-43">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh76-77_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh76-77-44">[42]</a></sup> with Ian Moore and <a href="/wiki/Storm_Thorgerson" title="Storm Thorgerson">Storm Thorgerson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[nb 3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning7_43-3"><a href="#cite_note-manning7-43">[41]</a></sup> During one trip, Barrett and another friend, Paul Charrier, ended up naked in the bath, reciting: "No rules, no rules".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning11_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning11-46">[43]</a></sup> That summer, as a consequence of the continuation of drug use, the band became absorbed in <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_Sant_Mat_movements" title="Contemporary Sant Mat movements">Sant Mat</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Sikh" title="Sikh">Sikh</a> sect. Storm Thorgerson (then living on Earlham Street) and Barrett went to a London hotel to meet the sect's <a href="/wiki/Guru" title="Guru">guru</a>; Thorgerson managed to join the sect, while Barrett, however, was deemed too young to join. Thorgerson perceives this as a deeply important event in Barrett's life, as he was intensely upset by the rejection. While living within proximity of his friends, Barrett decided to write more songs ("<a href="/wiki/Bike_(song)" title="Bike (song)">Bike</a>" was written around this time).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning10_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning10-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="London_Underground.2C_Blackhill_Enterprises_and_gigs">London Underground, Blackhill Enterprises and gigs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Syd_Barrett&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: London Underground, Blackhill Enterprises and gigs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Blackhill_Enterprises" title="Blackhill Enterprises">Blackhill Enterprises</a></div>
<p>While Pink Floyd began by playing cover versions of American <a href="/wiki/Rhythm_and_blues" title="Rhythm and blues">R&amp;B</a> songs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh73_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh73-47">[44]</a></sup> by 1966 they had carved out their own style of improvised rock and roll,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlake200845_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlake200845-48">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh99_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh99-49">[46]</a></sup> which drew as much from improvised <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh124_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh124-50">[47]</a></sup> After <a href="/wiki/Bob_Klose" title="Bob Klose">Bob Klose</a> departed from the band, the band's direction changed. However, the change was not instantaneous,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[nb 4]</a></sup> with more improvising on the guitars and keyboards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning10_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-manning10-37">[37]</a></sup> Mason reflected, "It always felt to me that most of the ideas were emanating from Syd at the time."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[nb 5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning10_37-3"><a href="#cite_note-manning10-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>At this time, Barrett's reading reputedly included: <a href="/wiki/Brothers_Grimm" title="Brothers Grimm">Grimm</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Grimms%27_Fairy_Tales" title="Grimms' Fairy Tales">Fairy Tales</a></i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tolkien" title="Tolkien">Tolkien</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hobbit" title="The Hobbit">The Hobbit</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lord_of_the_Rings" title="Lord of the Rings">Lord of the Rings</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Castaneda" title="Carlos Castaneda">Carlos Castaneda</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Teachings_of_Don_Juan" title="The Teachings of Don Juan">The Teachings of Don Juan</a></i>, and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iching" title="Iching">The I-Ching</a></i>. During this period, Barrett wrote most of the songs for Pink Floyd's <a href="/wiki/The_Piper_at_the_Gates_of_Dawn" title="The Piper at the Gates of Dawn">first album</a>, and also songs that would later appear on his solo albums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning14_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-manning14-54">[50]</a></sup> In 1966, a new rock concert venue, the <a href="/wiki/UFO_Club" title="UFO Club">UFO</a> (pronounced as "you-foe"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning19_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning19-57">[52]</a></sup> opened in London and quickly became a haven for British <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_music" title="Psychedelic music">psychedelic music</a>. Pink Floyd, the house band,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh86_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh86-51">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning19_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning19-57">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MCLM27_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-MCLM27-59">[54]</a></sup> was its most popular attraction and after making appearances at the rival <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Roundhouse" title="The Roundhouse">Roundhouse</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MCLM27_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-MCLM27-59">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning13_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning13-60">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh115_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh115-61">[56]</a></sup> became the most popular musical group of the "<a href="/wiki/UK_underground" title="UK underground">London Underground</a>" psychedelic music scene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pal001_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-pal001-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the end of 1966, Pink Floyd had gained a reliable management team in <a href="/wiki/Andrew_King_(music_manager)" title="Andrew King (music manager)">Andrew King</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Jenner" title="Peter Jenner">Peter Jenner</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning16_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning16-62">[57]</a></sup> Towards the end of October 1966, Pink Floyd, with King and Jenner, set up <a href="/wiki/Blackhill_Enterprises" title="Blackhill Enterprises">Blackhill Enterprises</a>, to manage the group's finances. Blackhill was staffed by lodgers Jenner found in his Edbrooke Road house, and among others, Barrett's flatmate, Peter Wynne Wilson (who became road manager, however, since he had more experience in lighting, he was also lighting assistant).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning15_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning15-63">[58]</a></sup> King and Jenner wanted to prepare some demo recordings for a possible record deal, so at the end of October, they booked a session at Thompson Private Recording Studio,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MCLM27_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-MCLM27-59">[54]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Hemel_Hempstead" title="Hemel Hempstead">Hemel Hempstead</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[nb 6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning15_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-manning15-63">[58]</a></sup> King said of the demos: "That was the first time I realised they were going to write all their own material, Syd just turned into a songwriter, it seemed like overnight."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning200628.E2.80.9329_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning200628.E2.80.9329-65">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>King and Jenner befriended American expatriate <a href="/wiki/Joe_Boyd" title="Joe Boyd">Joe Boyd</a>, the promoter of the <a href="/wiki/UFO_Club" title="UFO Club">UFO Club</a>, who was making a name for himself as one of the more important entrepreneurs on the British music scene. The newly hired booking agent, <a href="/wiki/Bryan_Morrison" title="Bryan Morrison">Bryan Morrison</a>, and Boyd had proposed sending in better quality recordings. From Morrison's agency the band played a gig outside London for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning17_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning17-66">[60]</a></sup> In November, the band performed the first (of many) strangely named concerts: Philadelic Music for Simian Hominids, a multimedia event arranged by the group's former landlord, Mike Leonard, at Hornsey College of Art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MCLM27_59-3"><a href="#cite_note-MCLM27-59">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning17_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning17-66">[60]</a></sup> They performed at the Free School<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh95_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh95-67">[61]</a></sup> for the following two weeks, before performing at the Psychodelphia Versus <a href="/wiki/Ian_Smith" title="Ian Smith">Ian Smith</a> event at the Roundhouse in December, arranged by the Majority Rule for Rhodesia Campaign, and an <a href="/wiki/Oxfam" title="Oxfam">Oxfam</a> benefit at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albert_Hall" title="Albert Hall">Albert Hall</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MCLM27_59-4"><a href="#cite_note-MCLM27-59">[54]</a></sup> (the band's biggest venue up to this point).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning17_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-manning17-66">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tonite_Lets_All_Make_Love_in_London"><i>Tonite Lets All Make Love in London</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Syd_Barrett&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Tonite Lets All Make Love in London">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tonite_Lets_All_Make_Love_in_London" title="Tonite Lets All Make Love in London">Tonite Lets All Make Love in London</a></div>
<p>At the beginning of 1967, Barrett was dating Jenny Spires (who would later marry future <a href="/wiki/Stars_(British_band)" title="Stars (British band)">Stars</a> member Jack Monck). However, unknown to Barrett, Spires had an affair with <a href="/wiki/Peter_Whitehead_(filmmaker)" title="Peter Whitehead (filmmaker)">Peter Whitehead</a>. Spires convinced Whitehead (who thought the band sounded like "bad <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Schoenberg" title="Arnold Schoenberg">Schoenberg</a>") to use Pink Floyd in a film about the <a href="/wiki/Swinging_London" title="Swinging London">swinging London</a> scene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning18_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning18-68">[62]</a></sup> So at the cost of £80, in January, Whitehead took the band into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Wood_(music_producer)" title="John Wood (music producer)">John Wood</a>'s Sound Techniques in Chelsea,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh123_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh123-69">[63]</a></sup> with promoter Joe Boyd in tow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning18_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning18-68">[62]</a></sup> Here, the band recorded a 16-minute version of "Interstellar Overdrive" and another composition, "Nick's Boogie".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning18_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-manning18-68">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh123_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh123-69">[63]</a></sup> Whitehead had filmed this recording, which was used in the film <i>Tonite Let's All Make Love in London</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh123_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh123-69">[63]</a></sup> and later on the video release of <i><a href="/wiki/London_%2766%E2%80%93%2767" title="London '66–'67">London '66–'67</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning18_68-3"><a href="#cite_note-manning18-68">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh123_69-3"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh123-69">[63]</a></sup> Whitehead later commented about the band that: "They were just completely welded together, just like a jazz group".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning18_68-4"><a href="#cite_note-manning18-68">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Piper_at_the_Gates_of_Dawn"><i>The Piper at the Gates of Dawn</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Syd_Barrett&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: The Piper at the Gates of Dawn">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Piper_at_the_Gates_of_Dawn" title="The Piper at the Gates of Dawn">The Piper at the Gates of Dawn</a></div>
<p>Boyd attempted to sign the band with <a href="/wiki/Polydor_Records" title="Polydor Records">Polydor Records</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh132_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh132-55">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toby31-32_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-toby31-32-70">[64]</a></sup> However, Morrison had convinced King and Jenner to try to start a bidding war between Polydor and <a href="/wiki/EMI" title="EMI">EMI</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toby31-32_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-toby31-32-70">[64]</a></sup> In late January, Boyd produced a recording session for the group,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh132_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh132-55">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning16_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning16-62">[57]</a></sup> with them returning to Sound Techniques in Chelsea again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[nb 7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh132_55-4"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh132-55">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MCLM28_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-MCLM28-73">[66]</a></sup> After the aforementioned bidding war idea was finished, Pink Floyd signed with EMI. Unusual for the time, the deal included recording an album, which meant the band had unlimited studio time at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EMI_Studios" title="EMI Studios">EMI Studios</a> in return for a smaller royalty percentage. The band then attempted to re-record "Arnold Layne", but the Boyd version from January was released instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning20_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning20-71">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>The band's first studio album, <a href="/wiki/The_Piper_at_the_Gates_of_Dawn" title="The Piper at the Gates of Dawn">The Piper at the Gates of Dawn</a>, was recorded intermittently between February and July 1967 in Studio 3 at Abbey Road Studios, and produced by former Beatles engineer <a href="/wiki/Norman_Smith_(record_producer)" title="Norman Smith (record producer)">Norman Smith</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning200634_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning200634-74">[67]</a></sup> At the same time, the <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">Beatles</a> were recording "<a href="/wiki/Lovely_Rita" title="Lovely Rita">Lovely Rita</a>" for their album, <i><a href="/wiki/Sgt._Pepper%27s_Lonely_Hearts_Club_Band" title="Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band">Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band</a></i>, in Studio 2. By the time the album was released on 4 August, "Arnold Layne" (which was released months earlier, on 11 March) had reached number 20 on the British singles charts<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-officialcharts1_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-officialcharts1-75">[68]</a></sup> (despite being banned by Radio London)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning20_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-manning20-71">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh141-142_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh141-142-76">[69]</a></sup> and the follow-up single, "<a href="/wiki/See_Emily_Play" title="See Emily Play">See Emily Play</a>", had done even better, peaking at number 6.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-officialcharts1_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-officialcharts1-75">[68]</a></sup> The album was successful in the UK, hitting number 6 on the British album charts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-officialcharts1_75-2"><a href="#cite_note-officialcharts1-75">[68]</a></sup> Their first three singles (including their third, "<a href="/wiki/Apples_and_Oranges_(song)" title="Apples and Oranges (song)">Apples and Oranges</a>"), were written by Barrett, who also was the principal visionary/author of their critically acclaimed 1967 debut album. Of the eleven songs on <i>Piper</i>, Barrett wrote eight and co-wrote another two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Departure_from_Pink_Floyd">Departure from Pink Floyd</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Syd_Barrett&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Departure from Pink Floyd">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Through late 1967 and early 1968, Barrett's behaviour became increasingly erratic and unpredictable, partly as a consequence of his reported heavy use of psychedelic drugs, most prominently <a href="/wiki/Lysergic_acid_diethylamide" title="Lysergic acid diethylamide">LSD</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pal001_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-pal001-9">[9]</a></sup> There is also much speculation that he suffered from schizophrenia. Once described as joyful, friendly, and extroverted, he became increasingly depressed and socially withdrawn, and experienced hallucinations, disorganized speech, memory lapses, intense mood swings, and periods of catatonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-5">[5]</a></sup> Although the changes began gradually, he went missing for a long weekend and, according to several friends including Rick Wright, came back "a completely different person."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-5">[5]</a></sup> One of the striking features of his change was the development of a blank, empty, dead-eyed stare. He was unable to recognize old friends that he had known for years, and often did not know where he was. At one point, while on a tour of the city of Los Angeles, Barrett is said to have exclaimed, "gee, it sure is nice to be in Las Vegas!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-5">[5]</a></sup> Many reports described him on stage, strumming one chord through the entire concert, or not playing at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Econ_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Econ-78">[71]</a></sup> At a show at <a href="/wiki/The_Fillmore" title="The Fillmore">The Fillmore</a> in San Francisco, during a performance of "Interstellar Overdrive", Barrett slowly detuned his guitar. The audience seemed to enjoy such antics, unaware of the rest of the band's consternation. Interviewed on <a href="/wiki/Pat_Boone" title="Pat Boone">Pat Boone</a>'s show during this tour, Syd's reply to Boone's questions was a "blank and totally mute stare," according to <a href="/wiki/Nick_Mason" title="Nick Mason">Nick Mason</a>, "Syd wasn't into moving his lips that day." Barrett exhibited similar behaviour during the band's first appearance on <a href="/wiki/Dick_Clark" title="Dick Clark">Dick Clark</a>'s popular TV show <i><a href="/wiki/American_Bandstand" title="American Bandstand">American Bandstand</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchaffner200513_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaffner200513-79">[72]</a></sup> Although surviving footage of this appearance shows Barrett miming his parts of the song competently,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChapman2010199_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChapman2010199-80">[73]</a></sup> during a group interview afterwards, when asked two questions by Clark, Barrett's answers were terse, almost to the point of rudeness (though, Clark noted, they had been flying non-stop from London to Los Angeles). During this time, Barrett would often forget to bring his guitar to sessions, damage equipment and occasionally was unable to hold his <a href="/wiki/Plectrum" title="Plectrum">pick</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillis2002102_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillis2002102-81">[74]</a></sup> Before a performance in late 1967, Barrett reportedly crushed <a href="/wiki/Methaqualone" title="Methaqualone">Mandrax</a> tranquilliser tablets and an entire tube of <a href="/wiki/Brylcreem" title="Brylcreem">Brylcreem</a> into his hair, which subsequently melted down his face under the heat of the stage lighting,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning200642_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning200642-82">[75]</a></sup> making him look like "a guttered candle".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaffner13-14_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schaffner13-14-83">[76]</a></sup> Nick Mason later disputed the Mandrax portion of this story, stating that "Syd would never waste good mandies".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tw009_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-tw009-84">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>During their UK tour with <a href="/wiki/Jimi_Hendrix" title="Jimi Hendrix">Jimi Hendrix</a> in November 1967, guitarist <a href="/wiki/David_O%27List" title="David O'List">David O'List</a> from <a href="/wiki/The_Nice" title="The Nice">The Nice</a> was called in to substitute for Barrett on several occasions when he was unable to perform or failed to appear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mason95-105_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mason95-105-85">[78]</a></sup> Sometime around Christmas, David Gilmour (Barrett's old school friend) was asked to join the band as a second guitarist to cover for Barrett, with the idea of retaining a five-member line-up of the band. For a handful of shows Gilmour played and sang while Barrett wandered around on stage, occasionally deciding to join in playing. The other band members soon grew tired of Barrett's antics and, on 26 January 1968, when Waters was driving on the way to a show at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southampton_University" title="Southampton University">Southampton University</a>, the band elected not to pick Barrett up: one person in the car said, "Shall we pick Syd up?" and another said, "Let's not bother."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlake2008112_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlake2008112-87">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toby45_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-toby45-88">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaffner14-15_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schaffner14-15-89">[82]</a></sup> As Barrett had, up until then, written the bulk of the band's material, the initial plan was to keep him in the group as a non-touring member—as <a href="/wiki/The_Beach_Boys" title="The Beach Boys">The Beach Boys</a> had done with <a href="/wiki/Brian_Wilson" title="Brian Wilson">Brian Wilson</a>—but this soon proved to be impractical.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toby45_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-toby45-88">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchaffner2005265_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaffner2005265-90">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaffner14_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schaffner14-91">[84]</a></sup> Gilmour subsequently became a full-time member of the band.</p>
<p>According to Roger Waters, Barrett came into what was to be their last practice session with a new song he had dubbed "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Have_You_Got_It_Yet%3F" title="Have You Got It Yet?">Have You Got It Yet?</a>" The song seemed simple enough when he first presented it, but it soon became impossibly difficult to learn and they eventually realised that while they were practising it, Barrett kept changing the arrangement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toby45_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-toby45-88">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaffner14_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schaffner14-91">[84]</a></sup> He would then play it again, with the arbitrary changes, and sing "Have you got it yet?" Eventually they realised they never would, and that they were simply bearing the brunt of Barrett's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Idiosyncratic" title="Idiosyncratic">idiosyncratic</a> sense of humour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tp001_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-tp001-92">[85]</a></sup> Waters had called it "a real act of mad genius".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toby45_88-3"><a href="#cite_note-toby45-88">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaffner14_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schaffner14-91">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>Barrett did not contribute material to the band after <i><a href="/wiki/A_Saucerful_of_Secrets" title="A Saucerful of Secrets">A Saucerful of Secrets</a></i> was released in 1968. Of the songs he wrote for Pink Floyd after <i>The Piper at the Gates of Dawn</i>, only one, "<a href="/wiki/Jugband_Blues" title="Jugband Blues">Jugband Blues</a>", made it to the band's second album; one, "Apples and Oranges", became a less-than-successful single; and two others, "<a href="/wiki/Scream_Thy_Last_Scream" title="Scream Thy Last Scream">Scream Thy Last Scream</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Vegetable_Man" title="Vegetable Man">Vegetable Man</a>", were never officially released until 2016 in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Early_Years_1965%E2%80%931972" title="The Early Years 1965–1972">The Early Years 1965–1972</a></i> box set, as they were deemed too dark and unsettling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-5">[5]</a></sup> Feeling guilty for ousting their friend, the members of Pink Floyd were unable to bring themselves to definitively tell Barrett that he was no longer in the band.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-5">[5]</a></sup> According to Rick Wright, who lived with Barrett at the time, Wright had the awful job of telling Barrett that he was going out to buy cigarettes while he went off to play a gig. He would return hours later to find Barrett in the same position, sometimes with a cigarette burned completely down between his fingers (an incident later referenced in Pink Floyd's <i>The Wall</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_5-6"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-5">[5]</a></sup> Emerging from catatonia and unaware that a long period of time had elapsed, Barrett would ask, "Have you got the cigarettes?".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_5-7"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-5">[5]</a></sup> Barrett supposedly spent time outside the recording studio, in the reception area,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaffner15_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schaffner15-93">[86]</a></sup> waiting to be invited in. He also showed up to a few gigs and glared at Gilmour. Barrett played slide guitar on "<a href="/wiki/Remember_a_Day" title="Remember a Day">Remember a Day</a>" (which had been first attempted during the <i>Piper</i> sessions), and also played on "<a href="/wiki/Set_the_Controls_for_the_Heart_of_the_Sun" title="Set the Controls for the Heart of the Sun">Set the Controls for the Heart of the Sun</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[87]</a></sup> On 6 April 1968, the group officially announced Barrett was no longer a member,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaffner15_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schaffner15-93">[86]</a></sup> the same day the band's contract with Blackhill Enterprises was terminated as the record label, considering Barrett to be the musical brains of the band, stayed with Barrett.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_5-8"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toby45_88-4"><a href="#cite_note-toby45-88">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solo_years_.281968.E2.80.9372.29">Solo years (1968–72)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Syd_Barrett&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Solo years (1968–72)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After leaving Pink Floyd, Barrett was out of the public eye for a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[88]</a></sup> Then, in 1969, at the behest of EMI and <a href="/wiki/Harvest_Records" title="Harvest Records">Harvest Records</a>, he embarked on a brief solo career, releasing two solo albums, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Madcap_Laughs" title="The Madcap Laughs">The Madcap Laughs</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Barrett_(album)" title="Barrett (album)">Barrett</a></i> (both 1970), and a single, "<a href="/wiki/Octopus_(Syd_Barrett_song)" title="Octopus (Syd Barrett song)">Octopus</a>". Some songs, "<a href="/wiki/Terrapin_(song)" title="Terrapin (song)">Terrapin</a>", "Maisie" and "<a href="/wiki/Bob_Dylan_Blues" title="Bob Dylan Blues">Bob Dylan Blues</a>", reflected Barrett's early interest in the blues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning20069_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning20069-96">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Madcap_Laughs"><i>The Madcap Laughs</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Syd_Barrett&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: The Madcap Laughs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Madcap_Laughs" title="The Madcap Laughs">The Madcap Laughs</a></div>
<p>After Barrett left Pink Floyd, Jenner followed suit. He led Barrett into EMI Studios to record some tracks<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MCLM3_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-MCLM3-97">[90]</a></sup> in May that would later be released on Barrett's first solo album, <i>The Madcap Laughs</i>. However, Jenner said: "I had seriously underestimated the difficulties of working with him".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning200670_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning200670-98">[91]</a></sup> By the sessions of June and July, most of the tracks were in better shape; however, shortly after the July sessions, Barrett broke up with girlfriend Lindsay Corner and went on a drive around Britain in his Mini, ending up in psychiatric care in Cambridge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning22_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning22-99">[92]</a></sup> During New Year 1969, a somewhat recovered Barrett had taken up tenancy in a flat on Egerton Gardens, <a href="/wiki/South_Kensington" title="South Kensington">South Kensington</a>, London, with the Post Modernist artist <a href="/wiki/Duggie_Fields" title="Duggie Fields">Duggie Fields</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning22_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning22-99">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[93]</a></sup> Here, Barrett's flat was so close to Gilmour's that Gilmour could look right into Barrett's kitchen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning22_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-manning22-99">[92]</a></sup> Deciding to return to music, Barrett contacted EMI and was passed to Malcolm Jones, the then-head of EMI's new <a href="/wiki/Progressive_rock" title="Progressive rock">prog rock</a> label, <a href="/wiki/Harvest_Records" title="Harvest Records">Harvest</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MCLM3_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-MCLM3-97">[90]</a></sup> (after Norman Smith<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MCLM4_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-MCLM4-101">[94]</a></sup> and Jenner declined to produce Barrett's record,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MCLM4_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-MCLM4-101">[94]</a></sup> Jones produced it).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning22_99-3"><a href="#cite_note-manning22-99">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MCLM4_101-2"><a href="#cite_note-MCLM4-101">[94]</a></sup> Barrett wanted to recover the Jenner-produced sessions recordings; several of the tracks were improved upon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MCLM3-4_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-MCLM3-4-102">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Jones-produced sessions started in April 1969 at EMI Studios. After the first of these sessions, Barrett brought in friends to help out: <a href="/wiki/Humble_Pie_(band)" title="Humble Pie (band)">Humble Pie</a> drummer, <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Shirley" title="Jerry Shirley">Jerry Shirley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jokers_Wild_(band)" title="Jokers Wild (band)">Jokers Wild</a> (Gilmour's old band) drummer, Willie Wilson. For the sessions, Gilmour played bass. Talking to Barrett wasn't easy, said Jones: "It was a case of following him, not playing with him. They were seeing and then playing so they were always a note behind".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning22_99-4"><a href="#cite_note-manning22-99">[92]</a></sup> A few tracks on the album feature overdubs by members of the band <a href="/wiki/Soft_Machine" title="Soft Machine">Soft Machine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning13_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning13-60">[55]</a></sup> During this time, Barrett also played guitar on the sessions for Soft Machine founder <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Ayers" title="Kevin Ayers">Kevin Ayers</a>' debut LP <i><a href="/wiki/Joy_of_a_Toy" title="Joy of a Toy">Joy of a Toy</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[96]</a></sup> although his performance on "Religious Experience" (later titled "<a href="/wiki/Singing_a_Song_in_the_Morning" title="Singing a Song in the Morning">Singing a Song in the Morning</a>") was not released until the album was reissued in 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning13_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-manning13-60">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palacios362_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-palacios362-104">[97]</a></sup> One time, Barrett had told his flatmate that he was going off "for an afternoon drive". However, he followed Pink Floyd to <a href="/wiki/Ibiza" title="Ibiza">Ibiza</a> (according to legend, he skipped check-ins and customs, ran onto the runway and attempted to flag down a jet). One of his friends, J. Ryan Eaves, bass player for the short-lived but influential Manchester band "York's Ensemble", later spotted him on a beach wearing messed-up clothes and with a carrier bag full of money. At this point, during the trip, Barrett had asked Gilmour for his help in the recording sessions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning22_99-5"><a href="#cite_note-manning22-99">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>After two of the Gilmour/Waters-produced sessions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParker2001iv_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParker2001iv-105">[98]</a></sup> they remade one track from the Soft Machine overdubs and recorded three tracks. These sessions came to a minor halt when Gilmour and Waters were mixing Pink Floyd's newly recorded album, <i><a href="/wiki/Ummagumma" title="Ummagumma">Ummagumma</a></i>, to Barrett's dismay. However, through the end of July, they managed to record three more tracks. The problem with the recording was that the songs were recorded as Barrett played them "live" in studio. On the released versions a number of them have false starts and commentaries from Barrett.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning22_99-6"><a href="#cite_note-manning22-99">[92]</a></sup> Despite the track being closer to complete and better produced, Gilmour and Waters left the Jones-produced track "Opel" off <i>Madcap</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning200671.E2.80.9372_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning200671.E2.80.9372-106">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gilmour later said of the sessions for <i>The Madcap Laughs</i>:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>[Sessions] were pretty tortuous and very rushed. We had very little time, particularly with <i>The Madcap Laughs</i>. Syd was very difficult, we got that very frustrated feeling: Look, it's your fucking career, mate. Why don't you get your finger out and do something? The guy was in trouble, and was a close friend for many years before then, so it really was the least one could do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[100]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Upon the album's release in January 1970, Malcolm Jones was shocked by the substandard musicianship on the Gilmour and Waters-produced songs: "I felt angry. It's like dirty linen in public and very unnecessary and unkind". Gilmour said: "Perhaps we were trying to show what Syd was really like. But perhaps we were trying to punish him". Waters was more positive: "Syd is a genius".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning23_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning23-108">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>Barrett said "It's quite nice but I'd be very surprised if it did anything if I were to drop dead. I don't think it would stand as my last statement." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning23_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning23-108">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Barrett"><i>Barrett</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Syd_Barrett&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Barrett">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Barrett_(album)" title="Barrett (album)">Barrett (album)</a></div>
<p>The second album, <i>Barrett</i>, was recorded more sporadically than the first,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[102]</a></sup> with sessions taking place between February and July 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning23_108-2"><a href="#cite_note-manning23-108">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-B2010_1-2_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-B2010_1-2-110">[103]</a></sup> The album was produced by <a href="/wiki/David_Gilmour" title="David Gilmour">David Gilmour</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning23_108-3"><a href="#cite_note-manning23-108">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manning_2006_61_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Manning_2006_61-111">[104]</a></sup> and featured Gilmour on bass guitar, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wright_(musician)" title="Richard Wright (musician)">Richard Wright</a> on keyboard and <a href="/wiki/Humble_Pie_(band)" title="Humble Pie (band)">Humble Pie</a> drummer <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Shirley" title="Jerry Shirley">Jerry Shirley</a>. The first two songs attempted were for Barrett to play and/or sing to an existing backing track. However, Gilmour thought they were losing the "Barrett-ness". One track ("Rats") was originally recorded with Barrett on his own. That would later be overdubbed by musicians, despite the changing tempos. Shirley said of Barrett's playing: "He would never play the same tune twice. Sometimes Syd couldn't play anything that made sense; other times what he'd play was absolute magic." At times Barrett, who experienced extreme <a href="/wiki/Synesthesia" title="Synesthesia">synesthesia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_5-9"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-5">[5]</a></sup> would say: "Perhaps we could make the middle darker and maybe the end a bit middle afternoonish. At the moment it's too windy and icy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning23_108-4"><a href="#cite_note-manning23-108">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>These sessions were happening while Pink Floyd had just begun to work on <i><a href="/wiki/Atom_Heart_Mother" title="Atom Heart Mother">Atom Heart Mother</a></i>. On various occasions, Barrett went to "spy" on the band as they recorded their album.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning23_108-5"><a href="#cite_note-manning23-108">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wright said of the <i>Barrett</i> sessions:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Doing Syd's record was interesting, but extremely difficult. Dave [Gilmour] and <a href="/wiki/Roger_Waters" title="Roger Waters">Roger</a> did the first one (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Madcap_Laughs" title="The Madcap Laughs">The Madcap Laughs</a></i>) and Dave and myself did the second one. But by then it was just trying to help Syd any way we could, rather than worrying about getting the best guitar sound. You could forget about that! It was just going into the studio and trying to get him to sing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[105]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Performances">Performances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Syd_Barrett&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Performances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Despite the numerous recording dates for his solo albums, Barrett undertook very little musical activity between 1968 and 1972 outside the studio. On 24 February 1970, he appeared on <a href="/wiki/John_Peel" title="John Peel">John Peel</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Broadcasting_Corporation" title="British Broadcasting Corporation">BBC</a> radio programme <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Top_Gear_(Music_show)" title="Top Gear (Music show)">Top Gear</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning23_108-6"><a href="#cite_note-manning23-108">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MCLM13_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-MCLM13-113">[106]</a></sup> playing five songs—only one of which had been previously released. Three would be re-recorded for the <i>Barrett</i> album, while the song "Two of a Kind" was a one-off performance (possibly written by Richard Wright).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[nb 8]</a></sup> Barrett was accompanied on this session by Gilmour and Shirley who played bass and percussion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning23_108-7"><a href="#cite_note-manning23-108">[101]</a></sup> respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[nb 9]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gilmour and Shirley also backed Barrett for his one and only live concert during this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manning_2006_61_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-Manning_2006_61-111">[104]</a></sup> The gig took place on 6 June 1970 at the Olympia Exhibition Hall as part of a <i>Music and Fashion Festival</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh270_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh270-118">[109]</a></sup> The trio performed four songs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manning_2006_61_111-2"><a href="#cite_note-Manning_2006_61-111">[104]</a></sup> "Terrapin", "Gigolo Aunt", "Effervescing Elephant" and "Octopus". Poor mixing left the vocals barely audible until part-way through the last number.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh270_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh270-118">[109]</a></sup> At the end of the fourth song, Barrett unexpectedly but politely put down his guitar and walked off the stage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manning_2006_61_111-3"><a href="#cite_note-Manning_2006_61-111">[104]</a></sup> The performance has been bootlegged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh270_118-2"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh270-118">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[110]</a></sup> Barrett made one last appearance on <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio" title="BBC Radio">BBC Radio</a>, recording three songs at their studios on 16 February 1971. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[nb 10]</a></sup> All three came from the <i>Barrett</i> album. After this session, he took a hiatus from his music career that lasted more than a year, although in an extensive interview with <a href="/wiki/Mick_Rock" title="Mick Rock">Mick Rock</a> and <i>Rolling Stone</i> in December, he discussed himself at length, showed off his new 12-string guitar, talked about touring with <a href="/wiki/Jimi_Hendrix" title="Jimi Hendrix">Jimi Hendrix</a> and stated that he was frustrated in terms of his musical work because of his inability to find anyone good to play with.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rs009_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-rs009-121">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_years_.281972.E2.80.932006.29">Later years (1972–2006)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Syd_Barrett&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Later years (1972–2006)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stars_and_final_recordings">Stars and final recordings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Syd_Barrett&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Stars and final recordings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Stars_(British_band)" title="Stars (British band)">Stars (British band)</a></div>
<p>In February 1972, after a few guest spots in Cambridge with ex-<a href="/wiki/Pink_Fairies" title="Pink Fairies">Pink Fairies</a> member <a href="/wiki/Twink_(musician)" title="Twink (musician)">Twink</a> on drums and <a href="/wiki/Jack_Monck" title="Jack Monck">Jack Monck</a> on bass using the name The Last Minute Put Together Boogie Band (backing visiting blues musician <a href="/wiki/Eddie_%22Guitar%22_Burns" title='Eddie "Guitar" Burns'>Eddie "Guitar" Burns</a> and also featuring Henry Cow guitarist <a href="/wiki/Fred_Frith" title="Fred Frith">Fred Frith</a>), the trio formed a short-lived band called <a href="/wiki/Stars_(British_band)" title="Stars (British band)">Stars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning26_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning26-122">[112]</a></sup> Though they were initially well received at gigs in the Dandelion coffee bar and the town's Market Square, one of their gigs at the <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Corn_Exchange" title="Cambridge Corn Exchange">Corn Exchange</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cambridge" title="Cambridge">Cambridge</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irghdxv_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irghdxv-123">[113]</a></sup> with the <a href="/wiki/MC5" title="MC5">MC5</a> proved to be disastrous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning24_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning24-124">[114]</a></sup> A few days after this final show, Twink recalled that Barrett stopped him on the street, showed him a scathing review of the gig they had played, and quit on the spot,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning24_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning24-124">[114]</a></sup> despite having played at least one subsequent gig at the same venue supporting <a href="/wiki/Nektar" title="Nektar">Nektar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tw009_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-tw009-84">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>Free from his EMI contract on 9 May 1972, Barrett signed a document that ended his association with Pink Floyd, and any financial interest in future recordings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DarkG400_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-DarkG400-125">[115]</a></sup> Barrett attended an informal jazz and poetry performance by <a href="/wiki/Pete_Brown" title="Pete Brown">Pete Brown</a> and former <a href="/wiki/Cream_(band)" title="Cream (band)">Cream</a> bassist <a href="/wiki/Jack_Bruce" title="Jack Bruce">Jack Bruce</a> in October 1973. Brown arrived at the show late, and saw that Bruce was already onstage, along with "a guitarist I vaguely recognised", playing the <a href="/wiki/Horace_Silver" title="Horace Silver">Horace Silver</a> tune "<a href="/wiki/Doodlin%27_(Horace_Silver_song)" title="Doodlin' (Horace Silver song)">Doodlin'</a>". Later in the show, Brown read out a poem, which he dedicated to Syd, because, "he's here in Cambridge, and he's one of the best songwriters in the country" when, to his surprise, the guitar player from earlier in the show stood up and said, "No I'm not".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalacios2010401_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalacios2010401-126">[116]</a></sup> By the end of 1973, Barrett had returned to live in London, staying at various hotels and, in December of that year, settling in at Chelsea Cloisters. He had little contact with others, apart from his regular visits to his management's offices to collect his royalties,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning26_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning26-122">[112]</a></sup> and the occasional visit from his sister Rosemary.</p>
<p>In August 1974,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning26_122-2"><a href="#cite_note-manning26-122">[112]</a></sup> Jenner persuaded Barrett to return to <a href="/wiki/Abbey_Road_Studios" title="Abbey Road Studios">Abbey Road Studios</a> in hope of recording another album. According to <a href="/wiki/John_Leckie" title="John Leckie">John Leckie</a>, who engineered these sessions, even at this point Syd still "looked like he did when he was younger..long haired".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParker2001194_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParker2001194-127">[117]</a></sup> The sessions lasted three days and consisted of blues rhythm tracks with tentative and disjointed guitar overdubs. Barrett recorded 11 tracks, the only one of which to be titled was "If You Go, Don't Be Slow". Once again, Barrett withdrew from the music industry, but this time for good. He sold the rights to his solo albums back to the record label and moved into a London hotel. During this period, several attempts to employ him as a <a href="/wiki/Record_producer" title="Record producer">record producer</a> (including one by <a href="/wiki/Jamie_Reid" title="Jamie Reid">Jamie Reid</a> on behalf of the <a href="/wiki/Sex_Pistols" title="Sex Pistols">Sex Pistols</a>, and another by <a href="/wiki/The_Damned_(band)" title="The Damned (band)">The Damned</a>, who wanted him to produce <a href="/wiki/Music_for_Pleasure_(The_Damned_album)" title="Music for Pleasure (The Damned album)">their second album</a>) were all fruitless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning1993_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning1993-128">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchaffner2005213_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaffner2005213-129">[119]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Withdrawal_to_Cambridge">Withdrawal to Cambridge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Syd_Barrett&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Withdrawal to Cambridge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 1978, when Barrett's money ran out, he moved back to Cambridge to live with his mother. He returned to live in London again in 1982, but lasted only a few weeks and soon returned to Cambridge for good. Barrett walked the 50 miles (80 km) from London to Cambridge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalacios2010414_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalacios2010414-130">[120]</a></sup> Until his death, Barrett received royalties from his work with Pink Floyd from each compilation and some of the live and studio albums and singles that featured his songs; Gilmour said that he "made sure the money got to [Barrett]." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cambridge-news2006_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-cambridge-news2006-131">[121]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1996, Barrett was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame" title="Rock and Roll Hall of Fame">Rock and Roll Hall of Fame</a> as a member of Pink Floyd, but he did not attend the ceremony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to a 2005 profile in the book <i>Madcap</i> by biographer and journalist Tim Willis, Barrett, who had reverted to using his original name of Roger, continued to live in his late mother's semi-detached home in Cambridge, and had returned to painting, creating large abstract canvases. He was also said to have been an avid gardener and his main point of contact with the outside world was his sister, Rosemary, who lived nearby. He was reclusive, and his physical health declined, as he suffered from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stomach_ulcer" title="Stomach ulcer">stomach ulcers</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_2_diabetes" title="Type 2 diabetes">type 2 diabetes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although Barrett had not appeared or spoken in public since the mid-1970s, time did little to diminish interest in his life and work. Reporters and fans still travelled to Cambridge to seek him out, despite his attempts to live a quiet life and public appeals from his family for people to leave him alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[124]</a></sup> Many photos of Barrett being harassed by <a href="/wiki/Paparazzi" title="Paparazzi">paparazzi</a> when walking or cycling from the 1980s until his death in 2006, have been published in various media. Apparently, Barrett did not like being reminded about his musical career and the other members of Pink Floyd had no direct contact with him. However, he did visit his sister's house in November 2001 to watch the BBC <i>Omnibus</i> documentary made about him – reportedly he found some of it "a bit noisy", enjoyed seeing Mike Leonard of Leonard's Lodgers again, calling him his "teacher", and enjoyed hearing "<a href="/wiki/See_Emily_Play" title="See Emily Play">See Emily Play</a>" again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>Barrett made a final public acknowledgement of his musical past in 2002, his first since the 1970s, when he autographed 320 copies of photographer <a href="/wiki/Mick_Rock" title="Mick Rock">Mick Rock</a>'s book <i>Psychedelic Renegades,</i> which contained a number of photos of Barrett. Rock was perhaps the last person in the music industry with whom Barrett kept in contact. In 1971, Rock conducted the final interview of Barrett before his retirement from the music industry a few years later, and Barrett subsequently turned up on Rock's London doorstep "four, maybe five times" for a cup of tea and conversation through 1978, before Barrett moved back to Cambridge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cavanagh_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-cavanagh-136">[126]</a></sup> They had not spoken in more than twenty years when Rock approached Barrett to autograph his photography book, and Barrett uncharacteristically agreed. Having reverted to his birth name "Roger" from his stage name "Syd" many years before, he had autographed the book simply "Barrett." <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cavanagh_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-cavanagh-136">[126]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_aftermath">Death and aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Syd_Barrett&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Death and aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>After suffering from diabetes for several years, Barrett died at home in Cambridge on 7 July 2006,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cambridge-news.co.uk_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-cambridge-news.co.uk-20">[20]</a></sup> aged 60. The cause of death was <a href="/wiki/Pancreatic_cancer" title="Pancreatic cancer">pancreatic cancer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatkinsonAnderson2001_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatkinsonAnderson2001-137">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[128]</a></sup> The occupation on his death certificate was "retired musician".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[129]</a></sup> He was <a href="/wiki/Cremation" title="Cremation">cremated</a>, with his ashes given to a family member or friend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[130]</a></sup> In 2006, his home in St. Margaret's Square, Cambridge, was put on the market and reportedly attracted considerable interest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[131]</a></sup> After over 100 showings, many by fans, it was sold to a French couple who bought it simply because they liked it; reportedly they knew nothing about Barrett.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[132]</a></sup> On 28 November 2006, Barrett's other possessions were sold at an auction at Cheffins auction house in Cambridge, raising £120,000 for charity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[133]</a></sup> Items sold included paintings, scrapbooks and everyday items that Barrett had decorated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[134]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/NME" title="NME">NME</a></i> produced a tribute issue to Barrett a week later with a photo of him on the cover. In an interview with <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sunday_Times" title="The Sunday Times">The Sunday Times</a></i>, Barrett's sister revealed that he had written a book:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>He read very deeply about the history of art and actually wrote an unpublished book about it, which I'm too sad to read at the moment. But he found his own mind so absorbing that he didn't want to be distracted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[135]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In response to the news of Barrett's death, fellow Pink Floyd bandmate David Gilmour said:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>We are very sad to say that Roger Keith Barrett – Syd – has passed away. Do find time to play some of Syd’s songs and to remember him as the madcap genius who made us all smile with his wonderfully eccentric songs about bikes, gnomes and scarecrows. His career was painfully short, yet he touched more people than he could ever know.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>According to local newspapers, Barrett left approximately £1.7 million to his two brothers and two sisters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[136]</a></sup> This sum was apparently largely acquired from royalties from Pink Floyd compilations and live recordings featuring songs he had written while with the band.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cambridge-news2006_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-cambridge-news2006-131">[121]</a></sup> A tribute concert called Games for May<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irghdxiv_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irghdxiv-147">[137]</a></sup> was held at the <a href="/wiki/Barbican_Centre" title="Barbican Centre">Barbican Centre</a>, London on 10 May 2007 with <a href="/wiki/Robyn_Hitchcock" title="Robyn Hitchcock">Robyn Hitchcock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Captain_Sensible" title="Captain Sensible">Captain Sensible</a>, <a href="/wiki/Damon_Albarn" title="Damon Albarn">Damon Albarn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chrissie_Hynde" title="Chrissie Hynde">Chrissie Hynde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Ayers" title="Kevin Ayers">Kevin Ayers</a> and his Pink Floyd bandmates performing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[138]</a></sup> A series of events called <i>The City Wakes</i> was held in Cambridge in October 2008 to celebrate Barrett's life, art and music. Barrett's sister, Rosemary Breen, supported this, the first-ever series of official events in memory of her brother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[139]</a></sup> After the festival's success, arts charity Escape Artists announced plans to create a centre in Cambridge, using art to help people suffering from mental health problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[140]</a></sup> A memorial bench has been placed in the Botanic Gardens in Cambridge and a more prominent tribute is planned in the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[141]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Syd_Barrett&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wish_You_Were_Here_sessions"><i>Wish You Were Here</i> sessions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Syd_Barrett&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Wish You Were Here sessions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Syd_Barrett_Abbey_Road_1975.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="270" data-file-width="275" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b6/Syd_Barrett_Abbey_Road_1975.jpg/220px-Syd_Barrett_Abbey_Road_1975.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/b/b6/Syd_Barrett_Abbey_Road_1975.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Syd_Barrett_Abbey_Road_1975.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Barrett visiting <a href="/wiki/Abbey_Road_Studios" title="Abbey Road Studios">Abbey Road Studios</a> on 5 June 1975</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Barrett had one noted reunion with the members of Pink Floyd, in 1975 during the recording sessions for <i><a href="/wiki/Wish_You_Were_Here_(Pink_Floyd_album)" title="Wish You Were Here (Pink Floyd album)">Wish You Were Here</a></i>. He attended the Abbey Road session unannounced, and watched the band working on the final mix of "<a href="/wiki/Shine_On_You_Crazy_Diamond" title="Shine On You Crazy Diamond">Shine On You Crazy Diamond</a>" — a song that happened to be about Barrett. By that time, the 29-year-old Barrett had become quite overweight, had shaved off all of his hair (including his eyebrows), and his ex-bandmates did not at first recognise him. Barrett's behaviour at the session was erratic; he spent part of the session brushing his teeth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning1_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning1-153">[143]</a></sup> Roger Waters finally managed to ask him what he thought of the song and he simply said "sounds a bit old".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning1_153-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning1-153">[143]</a></sup> He briefly attended the reception for Gilmour's wedding to <a href="/wiki/Ginger_Gilmour" title="Ginger Gilmour">Ginger</a> that immediately followed the recording sessions; however, he left early without saying goodbye.</p>
