<p>Apart from a brief encounter between Waters and Barrett in <a href="/wiki/Harrods" title="Harrods">Harrods</a> a couple of years later<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning24_124-2"><a href="#cite_note-manning24-124">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalacios2010412_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalacios2010412-154">[144]</a></sup> (during which, when Barrett saw Waters he ran outside, dropping his bags full of sweets in the process),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning24_124-3"><a href="#cite_note-manning24-124">[114]</a></sup> this was the last time any member of Pink Floyd saw him. A reflection on the day appears in Nick Mason's book <i><a href="/wiki/Inside_Out:_A_Personal_History_of_Pink_Floyd" title="Inside Out: A Personal History of Pink Floyd">Inside Out: A Personal History of Pink Floyd</a></i>. A reference to this reunion also appears in the film <i><a href="/wiki/Pink_Floyd_%E2%80%93_The_Wall" title="Pink Floyd – The Wall">The Wall</a></i>, where the character Pink, played by <a href="/wiki/Bob_Geldof" title="Bob Geldof">Bob Geldof</a>, shaves his body hair after having a mental breakdown, just as Barrett had.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compilations">Compilations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Syd_Barrett&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Compilations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1988, EMI Records (after constant pressure from Malcolm Jones)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning2_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning2-155">[145]</a></sup> released an album of Barrett's studio out-takes and previously unreleased material recorded from 1968 to 1970 under the title <i><a href="/wiki/Opel_(album)" title="Opel (album)">Opel</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AM_Opel_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-AM_Opel-156">[146]</a></sup> The disc was originally set to include the unreleased Barrett Pink Floyd songs "Scream Thy Last Scream" and "Vegetable Man", which had been remixed for the album by Jones,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning2_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning2-155">[145]</a></sup> but the band pulled the two songs<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2006186_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2006186-157">[147]</a></sup> before <i>Opel</i> was finalised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchaffner2005116.E2.80.93117_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaffner2005116.E2.80.93117-158">[148]</a></sup> In 1993 EMI issued another release, <i><a href="/wiki/Crazy_Diamond" title="Crazy Diamond">Crazy Diamond</a></i>, a boxed set of all three albums, each with further out-takes from his solo sessions that illustrated Barrett's inability/refusal to play a song the same way twice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AM_CD_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-AM_CD-159">[149]</a></sup> EMI also released <i><a href="/wiki/The_Best_of_Syd_Barrett:_Wouldn%27t_You_Miss_Me%3F" title="The Best of Syd Barrett: Wouldn't You Miss Me?">The Best of Syd Barrett: Wouldn't You Miss Me?</a></i> in the UK on 16 April 2001 and in the US on 11 September 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WYMM_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-WYMM-160">[150]</a></sup> This was the first time his song "<a href="/wiki/Bob_Dylan_Blues" title="Bob Dylan Blues">Bob Dylan Blues</a>" was officially released, taken from a demo tape that Gilmour had kept after an early 1970s session.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WYMM_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-WYMM-160">[150]</a></sup> Gilmour kept the tape, which also contains the unreleased "Living Alone" from the <i>Barrett</i> sessions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rp001_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-rp001-161">[151]</a></sup> In October 2010 Harvest/EMI and Capitol Records released <i><a href="/wiki/An_Introduction_to_Syd_Barrett" title="An Introduction to Syd Barrett">An Introduction to Syd Barrett</a></i>—a collection of both his Pink Floyd and remastered solo work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AM_Intro_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-AM_Intro-162">[152]</a></sup> The 2010 compilation <i>An Introduction to Syd Barrett</i> includes the downloadable bonus track "Rhamadan", a 20-minute track recorded at one of Syd's earliest solo sessions, in May 1968. In 2011, it was announced that a vinyl double album version would be issued for <a href="/wiki/Record_Store_Day" title="Record Store Day">Record Store Day</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[155]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pink_Floyd_bootleg_recordings" title="Pink Floyd bootleg recordings">Bootleg</a> editions of Barrett's live and solo material exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[157]</a></sup> For years the "off air" recordings of the BBC sessions with Barrett's Pink Floyd circulated, until an engineer who had taken a tape of the early Pink Floyd gave it back to the BBC—which played it during a tribute to <a href="/wiki/John_Peel" title="John Peel">John Peel</a> on their website. During this tribute, the first Peel programme (<i>Top Gear</i>) was aired in its entirety. This show featured the 1967 live versions of "<a href="/wiki/Flaming_(song)" title="Flaming (song)">Flaming</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Set_the_Controls_for_the_Heart_of_the_Sun" title="Set the Controls for the Heart of the Sun">Set the Controls for the Heart of the Sun</a>", and a brief 90-second snippet of the instrumental "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reaction_in_G" title="Reaction in G">Reaction in G</a>". In 2012, engineer Andy Jackson said he had found "a huge box of assorted tapes", in Mason's possession, containing versions of R&amp;B songs that (the Barrett-era) Pink Floyd played in their early years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[158]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Creative_impact_and_technical_innovation">Creative impact and technical innovation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Syd_Barrett&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Creative impact and technical innovation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BarrettHarmonyGuitar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/BarrettHarmonyGuitar.jpg/280px-BarrettHarmonyGuitar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/BarrettHarmonyGuitar.jpg/420px-BarrettHarmonyGuitar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/BarrettHarmonyGuitar.jpg/560px-BarrettHarmonyGuitar.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BarrettHarmonyGuitar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Barrett's first acoustic guitar</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Syd_Barrett_Guitar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Syd_Barrett_Guitar.jpg/180px-Syd_Barrett_Guitar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Syd_Barrett_Guitar.jpg/270px-Syd_Barrett_Guitar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Syd_Barrett_Guitar.jpg/360px-Syd_Barrett_Guitar.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Syd_Barrett_Guitar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Barrett's Mirrored Fender Esquire</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Barrett wrote most of Pink Floyd's early material. He was also an innovative guitarist, using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extended_techniques" title="Extended techniques">extended techniques</a> and exploring the musical and sonic possibilities of <a href="/wiki/Consonance_and_dissonance" title="Consonance and dissonance">dissonance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Distortion" title="Distortion">distortion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Audio_feedback" title="Audio feedback">feedback</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Echo_machine" title="Echo machine">echo machine</a>, tapes and other effects; his experimentation was partly inspired by free improvisation guitarist <a href="/wiki/Keith_Rowe" title="Keith Rowe">Keith Rowe</a> of the group <a href="/wiki/AMM_(group)" title="AMM (group)">AMM</a>, active at the time in London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPalacios2010101_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPalacios2010101-169">[159]</a></sup> One of Barrett's trademarks was playing his guitar through an old echo box while sliding a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zippo_lighter" title="Zippo lighter">Zippo lighter</a> up and down the fret-board to create the mysterious, otherworldly sounds that became associated with the group. Barrett was known to have used <a href="/wiki/Binson" title="Binson">Binson</a> delay units to achieve his trademark echo sounds. <a href="/wiki/Daevid_Allen" title="Daevid Allen">Daevid Allen</a>, founder member of Soft Machine and <a href="/wiki/Gong_(band)" title="Gong (band)">Gong</a>, has cited Barrett's use of slide guitar with echo as a key inspiration for his own "glissando guitar" style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>His recordings both with Pink Floyd and in later solo albums were delivered with a strongly British-accented vocal delivery, specifically that of southern-England. He was described by Guardian writer Nick Kent as having a "quintessential English style of vocal projection".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[161]</a></sup> David Bowie was quoted as saying that Barrett, along with <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Newley" title="Anthony Newley">Anthony Newley</a>, was the first person he had heard sing rock or pop music with a British accent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[162]</a></sup></p>
<p>Barrett's free-form sequences of "sonic carpets" pioneered a new way to play the rock guitar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Den001_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-Den001-173">[163]</a></sup> He played several different guitars during his tenure, including an old <a href="/wiki/Harmony_Company" title="Harmony Company">Harmony</a> hollowbody electric, a Harmony acoustic, a Fender acoustic, a single-coil <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Danelectro_59_DC" title="Danelectro 59 DC">Danelectro 59 DC</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[164]</a></sup> several different <a href="/wiki/Fender_Telecaster" title="Fender Telecaster">Fender Telecasters</a> and a white <a href="/wiki/Fender_Stratocaster" title="Fender Stratocaster">Fender Stratocaster</a> in late 1967. A silver <a href="/wiki/Fender_Esquire" title="Fender Esquire">Fender Esquire</a> with mirrored discs glued to the body<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh126_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh126-175">[165]</a></sup> was the guitar he was most often associated with and the guitar he "felt most close to."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rs009_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-rs009-121">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Musical_and_pop_culture_influence">Musical and pop culture influence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Syd_Barrett&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Musical and pop culture influence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many artists have acknowledged Barrett's influence on their work. <a href="/wiki/Paul_McCartney" title="Paul McCartney">Paul McCartney</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pete_Townshend" title="Pete Townshend">Pete Townshend</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning27_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning27-176">[166]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Blur_(band)" title="Blur (band)">Blur</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning32_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning32-178">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian2006_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardian2006-179">[169]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Ayers" title="Kevin Ayers">Kevin Ayers</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning28_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning28-180">[170]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gong_(band)" title="Gong (band)">Gong</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning28_180-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning28-180">[170]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marc_Bolan" title="Marc Bolan">Marc Bolan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning32_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning32-178">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning29_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning29-181">[171]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tangerine_Dream" title="Tangerine Dream">Tangerine Dream</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManning2006285.E2.80.93286_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManning2006285.E2.80.93286-182">[172]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Julian_Cope" title="Julian Cope">Julian Cope</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning30_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning30-183">[173]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/David_Bowie" title="David Bowie">David Bowie</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning32_178-2"><a href="#cite_note-manning32-178">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning29_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning29-181">[171]</a></sup> were inspired by Barrett; <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Page" title="Jimmy Page">Jimmy Page</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brian_Eno" title="Brian Eno">Brian Eno</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-luckymojo1_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-luckymojo1-184">[174]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/The_Damned_(band)" title="The Damned (band)">The Damned</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning1993_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning1993-128">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchaffner2005214_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaffner2005214-185">[175]</a></sup> all expressed interest in working with him at some point during the 1970s. Bowie recorded a cover of "<a href="/wiki/See_Emily_Play" title="See Emily Play">See Emily Play</a>" on his 1973 album <i><a href="/wiki/Pin_Ups" title="Pin Ups">Pin Ups</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[176]</a></sup> The track "Grass", from <a href="/wiki/XTC" title="XTC">XTC</a>'s album <i><a href="/wiki/Skylarking" title="Skylarking">Skylarking</a></i> was influenced when <a href="/wiki/Andy_Partridge" title="Andy Partridge">Andy Partridge</a> let fellow band member <a href="/wiki/Colin_Moulding" title="Colin Moulding">Colin Moulding</a> borrow his Barrett records. <a href="/wiki/Robyn_Hitchcock" title="Robyn Hitchcock">Robyn Hitchcock</a>'s career was dedicated to being Barrett-esque; he even played "Dominoes" for the 2001 BBC documentary <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pink_Floyd_and_Syd_Barrett_Story" title="The Pink Floyd and Syd Barrett Story">The Pink Floyd and Syd Barrett Story</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning30_183-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning30-183">[173]</a></sup></p>
<p>Barrett also had an influence on <a href="/wiki/Alternative_rock" title="Alternative rock">alternative</a> and <a href="/wiki/Punk_rock" title="Punk rock">punk</a> music in general. According to critic <a href="/wiki/John_Harris_(critic)" title="John Harris (critic)">John Harris</a>:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>To understand his place in modern music you probably have to first go back to punk rock and its misguided attempt to kick aside what remained of the psychedelic 1960s. Given that the Clash and <a href="/wiki/Sex_Pistols" title="Sex Pistols">Sex Pistols</a> had made brutal social commentary obligatory, there seemed little room for any of the creative exotica that had defined the Love Decade - until, slowly but surely, singing about dead-end lives and dole queues began to pall, and at least some of the previous generation were rehabilitated. Barrett was the best example: having crashed out of <a href="/wiki/Pink_Floyd" title="Pink Floyd">Pink Floyd</a> before the advent of indulgent "progressive" rock, and succumbed to a fate that appealed to the punk generation's nihilism, he underwent a revival."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[177]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Pink Floyd in their earliest, Syd Barrett-fronted iteration, they were practically a proto-punk band, the sinister, serrated riffs of “Lucifer Sam” and “Interstellar Overdrive” serving as a mainline to future avant-rock outfits like Can, Hawkwind, and Pere Ubu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[178]</a></sup></p>
<p>Barrett's decline had a profound effect on Roger Waters' songwriting, and the theme of mental illness permeated Pink Floyd's later albums, particularly 1973's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dark_Side_of_the_Moon" title="The Dark Side of the Moon">The Dark Side of the Moon</a></i> and 1975's <i><a href="/wiki/Wish_You_Were_Here_(Pink_Floyd_album)" title="Wish You Were Here (Pink Floyd album)">Wish You Were Here</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaffner16_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schaffner16-189">[179]</a></sup> which was a deliberate and affectionate tribute to Barrett, the song, "Shine on You Crazy Diamond",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[180]</a></sup> and also 1979's <i><a href="/wiki/The_Wall" title="The Wall">The Wall</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaffner16_189-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schaffner16-189">[179]</a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/Wish_You_Were_Here_(Pink_Floyd_song)" title="Wish You Were Here (Pink Floyd song)">Wish You Were Here</a>", partly about Barrett,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaffner18_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schaffner18-191">[181]</a></sup> borrows imagery of a "steel rail" from Barrett's solo song, "If It's in You," from <i>The Madcap Laughs</i> album.</p>
<p>In 1987, an album of Barrett cover songs called <i><a href="/wiki/Beyond_the_Wildwood" title="Beyond the Wildwood">Beyond the Wildwood</a></i> was released. The album was a collection of cover songs from Barrett's tenure with Pink Floyd and from his solo career. Artists appearing were UK and US <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indie_band" title="Indie band">indie bands</a> including <a href="/wiki/The_Shamen" title="The Shamen">The Shamen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Opal_(band)" title="Opal (band)">Opal</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Soup_Dragons" title="The Soup Dragons">The Soup Dragons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Plasticland" title="Plasticland">Plasticland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[182]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other artists who have written tributes to Barrett include his contemporary <a href="/wiki/Kevin_Ayers" title="Kevin Ayers">Kevin Ayers</a>, who wrote "O Wot a Dream" in his honour (Barrett provided guitar to an early version of Ayers' song "Religious Experience: Singing a Song in the Morning").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning13_60-3"><a href="#cite_note-manning13-60">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palacios362_104-1"><a href="#cite_note-palacios362-104">[97]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robyn_Hitchcock" title="Robyn Hitchcock">Robyn Hitchcock</a> has covered many of his songs live and on record and paid homage to his forebear with the song "(Feels Like) 1974". Phish covered "Bike", "No Good Trying", "Love You", "Baby Lemonade" and "Terrapin". The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Television_Personalities_(band)" title="Television Personalities (band)">Television Personalities</a>' single "I Know Where Syd Barrett Lives"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian2006_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-guardian2006-179">[169]</a></sup> from their 1981 album <i>And Don't the Kids Love It</i> is another tribute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[nb 11]</a></sup> In 2008, <a href="/wiki/The_Trash_Can_Sinatras" title="The Trash Can Sinatras">The Trash Can Sinatras</a> released a single in tribute to the life and work of Syd Barrett called "Oranges and Apples", from their 2009 album <i>In the Music</i>. Proceeds from the single go to the Syd Barrett Trust in support of arts in mental health.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Johnny_Depp" title="Johnny Depp">Johnny Depp</a> showed interest in a biographical film based on Barrett's life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[184]</a></sup> Barrett is portrayed briefly in the opening scene of <a href="/wiki/Tom_Stoppard" title="Tom Stoppard">Tom Stoppard</a>'s play <i><a href="/wiki/Rock_%27n%27_Roll_(play)" title="Rock 'n' Roll (play)">Rock 'n' Roll</a></i> (2006), performing "Golden Hair". His life and music, including the disastrous <a href="/wiki/Syd_Barrett#Later_years_.281972.E2.80.932006.29" title="Syd Barrett">Cambridge Corn Exchange concert</a> and his later reclusive lifestyle, are a recurring motif in the work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[186]</a></sup> Barrett died during the play's run in London.</p>
<p>In 2016, in correspondence of the 70th Anniversary birthday, The Theatre of the Absurd, an Italian independent artists group, published a short movie in honor of Syd Barrett named <a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=GlzfVtwCnHQ" rel="nofollow"><i>Eclipse</i></a>, with actor-director Edgar Blake in the role of Syd. Some footage from this movie was also showed at the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Syd_Barrett_-_A_Celebration&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Syd Barrett - A Celebration (page does not exist)">Syd Barrett - A Celebration</a> during Men on the Border's tribute: the show tooks place at the Cambridge Corn Exchange, with the participation of Syd's family and old friends.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Statue">Statue</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Syd_Barrett&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Statue">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In October 2015, a proposed statue of Barrett to be erected in the foyer of <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Corn_Exchange" title="Cambridge Corn Exchange">Cambridge Corn Exchange</a> was announced. With £10,000 funded by <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_City_Council" title="Cambridge City Council">Cambridge City Council</a>, the unveiling is planned for 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[187]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Syd_Barrett&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There has been much speculation concerning Barrett's psychological well-being. His family has denied that he suffered from mental illness (despite displaying an almost textbook case of schizophrenia),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_5-10"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-5">[5]</a></sup> but in a 2016 interview, his sister Rosemary Breen said that his mind was brilliant, bordering on that seen in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asperger%27s_syndrome" title="Asperger's syndrome">Asperger's syndrome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_5-11"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[188]</a></sup> Barrett's use of psychedelic drugs, especially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LSD" title="LSD">LSD</a>, during the 1960s is well documented, and there were theories he subsequently suffered from <a href="/wiki/Schizophrenia" title="Schizophrenia">schizophrenia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tp001_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-tp001-92">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rst001_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-rst001-200">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sch001_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-sch001-201">[190]</a></sup> Rick Wright asserts that Syd's problems stemmed from a massive overdose of acid, as the change in his personality and behavior came on quite suddenly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_5-12"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-5">[5]</a></sup> However, Roger Waters maintains that Syd suffered "without a doubt" from schizophrenia<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_5-13"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-5">[5]</a></sup> and in an article published in 2006, in response to notions that Barrett's problems came from the drug, David Gilmour was quoted as saying:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>In my opinion, his nervous breakdown would have happened anyway. It was a deep-rooted thing. But I'll say the <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_experience" title="Psychedelic experience">psychedelic experience</a> might well have acted as a catalyst. Still, I just don't think he could deal with the vision of success and all the things that went with it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[191]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Many stories of Barrett's erratic behaviour off stage as well as on are also well-documented. In <i>Saucerful of Secrets: The Pink Floyd Odyssey</i>, author <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Schaffner" title="Nicholas Schaffner">Nicholas Schaffner</a> interviewed people who knew Barrett before and during his Pink Floyd days. These included friends Peter and Susan Wynne-Wilson, artist <a href="/wiki/Duggie_Fields" title="Duggie Fields">Duggie Fields</a> (with whom Barrett shared a flat during the late 1960s), June Bolan and <a href="/wiki/Storm_Thorgerson" title="Storm Thorgerson">Storm Thorgerson</a>, among others.</p>
<p>"For June Bolan, the alarm bells began to sound only when Syd kept his girlfriend under lock and key for three days, occasionally shoving a ration of biscuits under the door."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchaffner200577_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaffner200577-203">[192]</a></sup> A claim of cruelty against Barrett committed by the groupies and hangers-on who frequented his apartment during this period was described by writer and critic <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Meades" title="Jonathan Meades">Jonathan Meades</a>. "I went [to Barrett's flat] to see Harry and there was this terrible noise. It sounded like heating pipes shaking. I said, 'What's up?' and he sort of giggled and said, 'That's Syd having a <a href="/wiki/Bad_trip" title="Bad trip">bad trip</a>. We put him in the linen cupboard'".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning33_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning33-204">[193]</a></sup> Storm Thorgerson responded to this claim by stating "I do not remember locking Syd up in a cupboard. It sounds to me like pure fantasy, like Jonathan Meades was on dope himself."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning33_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning33-204">[193]</a></sup> Other friends assert that Barrett's infamous flatmates, "Mad Jock" and "Mad Sue," believed that acid held all the answers and thought of Barrett as a genius or "god," and were spiking his morning coffee with LSD every day without his knowledge, leaving him in a never-ending trip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_5-14"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-5">[5]</a></sup> He was later rescued from that flat by friends and moved elsewhere, but his erratic behavior continued.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_5-15"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-5">[5]</a></sup> Watkinson and Anderson included quotes from a story told to them by Thorgerson that underscored how volatile Barrett could be. "On one occasion, I had to pull him off Lindsay (Barrett's girlfriend at the time) because he was beating her over the head with a <a href="/wiki/Mandolin" title="Mandolin">mandolin</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWatkinsonAnderson200183_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWatkinsonAnderson200183-205">[194]</a></sup> On one occasion, Barrett threw a woman called Gilly across the room, because she refused to go to Gilmour's house.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning24_124-4"><a href="#cite_note-manning24-124">[114]</a></sup> These strange behaviors contradicted Barrett's usually gentle nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_5-16"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-5">[5]</a></sup> According to Gilmour in an interview with <a href="/wiki/Nick_Kent" title="Nick Kent">Nick Kent</a>, the other members of Pink Floyd approached psychiatrist <a href="/wiki/R._D._Laing" title="R. D. Laing">R. D. Laing</a> with the 'Barrett problem'. After hearing a tape of a Barrett conversation, Laing declared him "incurable".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchaffner2005106.E2.80.93107_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaffner2005106.E2.80.93107-207">[196]</a></sup></p>
<p>After Barrett died, his sister Rosemary insisted that Barrett neither suffered from mental illness nor received treatment for it at any time since they resumed regular contact in the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wil002_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-wil002-208">[197]</a></sup> She allowed that he did spend some time in a private "home for lost souls"—Greenwoods in Essex—but claimed there was no formal therapy programme there. Some years later, Barrett apparently agreed to sessions with a psychiatrist at <a href="/wiki/Fulbourn_Hospital" title="Fulbourn Hospital">Fulbourn psychiatric hospital</a> in Cambridge, but Breen claimed that neither medication nor therapy was considered appropriate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wil002_208-1"><a href="#cite_note-wil002-208">[197]</a></sup> His sister denied he was a recluse or that he was vague about his past:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Roger may have been a bit selfish—or rather self-absorbed—but when people called him a recluse they were really only projecting their own disappointment. He knew what they wanted, but he wasn't willing to give it to them.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Barrett, she said, took up photography and sometimes they went to the seaside together. She also said he took a keen interest in art and horticulture and continued to devote himself to painting:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Quite often he took the train on his own to London to look at the major art collections—and he loved flowers. He made regular trips to the Botanic Gardens and to the <a href="/wiki/Dahlia" title="Dahlia">dahlias</a> at <a href="/wiki/Anglesey_Abbey" title="Anglesey Abbey">Anglesey Abbey</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Lode,_Cambridgeshire" title="Lode, Cambridgeshire">Lode</a>. But of course, his passion was his painting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wil002_208-2"><a href="#cite_note-wil002-208">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wil001_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-wil001-209">[198]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Despite his relationships with various women, such as Libby Gausden, Lindsay Korner, Jenny Spires and Iggy the Eskimo,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[199]</a></sup> Barrett never married or had children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[200]</a></sup> He was briefly engaged to marry Gayla Pinion and planned to relocate to <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[201]</a></sup> but the marriage never happened.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Syd_Barrett&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<dl>
<dt>Footnotes</dt>
</dl>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-38">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Barrett devised the name "Pink Floyd" by juxtaposing the first names of <a href="/wiki/Pink_Anderson" title="Pink Anderson">Pink Anderson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Floyd_Council" title="Floyd Council">Floyd Council</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh53_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh53-35">[35]</a></sup> whom he had read about in a sleeve note for a 1962 <a href="/wiki/Blind_Boy_Fuller" title="Blind Boy Fuller">Blind Boy Fuller</a> album: "Curley Weaver and Fred McMullen, [...] Pink Anderson or Floyd Council—these were a few amongst the many blues singers who were to be heard in the rolling hills of the Piedmont, or meandering with the streams through the wooded valleys."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning10_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning10-37">[37]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-42">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">They recorded a cover of <a href="/wiki/Slim_Harpo" title="Slim Harpo">Slim Harpo</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/I%27m_a_King_Bee" title="I'm a King Bee">I'm a King Bee</a>", and three Barrett originals: "Double O Bo", "Butterfly" and "Lucy Leave".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh65_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh65-40">[39]</a></sup> "Double O Bo" and "Lucy Leave" survive as vinyl <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acetates" title="Acetates">acetates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh65_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh65-40">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning8_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning8-41">[40]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-45">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">While under the influence of the acid, Barrett had placed an orange, a plum and a matchbox into a corner, while staring at the fruit, which he claimed symbolised "Venus and Jupiter".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning7_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning7-43">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh76-77_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh76-77-44">[42]</a></sup> Thorgerson later used this imagery, by adding the previously mentioned items to the cover of the double album combination of Barrett's solo albums, <i><a href="/wiki/Syd_Barrett_(album)" title="Syd Barrett (album)">Syd Barrett</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning7_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-manning7-43">[41]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-53"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-53">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The band were still playing R&amp;B hits as late as early 1966,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh86_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh86-51">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh104_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh104-52">[49]</a></sup> however, mixed in with several original songs: "Let's Roll Another One", "Lucy Leave", "Butterfly", "Remember Me" and "Walk with Me Sydney".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh86_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh86-51">[48]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-56"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-56">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Barrett, frequently at his Earlham Street residence, played <a href="/wiki/The_Mothers_of_Invention" title="The Mothers of Invention">The Mothers of Invention</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Freak_Out!" title="Freak Out!">Freak Out!</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/The_Byrds" title="The Byrds">The Byrds</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/Fifth_Dimension_(album)" title="Fifth Dimension (album)">Fifth Dimension</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/The_Fugs" title="The Fugs">The Fugs</a>' and <a href="/wiki/Love_(band)" title="Love (band)">Love</a>'s debut albums,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning14_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning14-54">[50]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">The Beatles</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/Revolver_(Beatles_album)" title="Revolver (Beatles album)">Revolver</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh132_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh132-55">[51]</a></sup> repeatedly. All these albums were connected by their proto-psychedelic feel, which had begun to guide Barrett's songs, as much as R&amp;B had, previously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning14_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning14-54">[50]</a></sup> "<a href="/wiki/Interstellar_Overdrive" title="Interstellar Overdrive">Interstellar Overdrive</a>" (included into the band's setlist from autumn), for example, was inspired by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riff" title="Riff">riff</a> from Love's "<a href="/wiki/My_Little_Red_Book" title="My Little Red Book">My Little Red Book</a>", the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_form_jazz" title="Free form jazz">free-form</a> section (and also, "<a href="/wiki/Pow_R._Toc_H." title="Pow R. Toc H.">Pow R. Toc H.</a>") was inspired by <a href="/wiki/Frank_Zappa" title="Frank Zappa">Frank Zappa</a>'s free-form freak-outs and The Byrds' "<a href="/wiki/Eight_Miles_High" title="Eight Miles High">Eight Miles High</a>". The Kinks' "<a href="/wiki/Sunny_Afternoon" title="Sunny Afternoon">Sunny Afternoon</a>" was an important influence on Barrett's songwriting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning14_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-manning14-54">[50]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The demo recordings consist of "I Get Stoned" (aka "Stoned Alone"), "Let's Roll Another One", "Lucy Leave" and a 15-minute version of "Interstellar Overdrive".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning15_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-manning15-63">[58]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-72"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-72">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Sound Techniques session resulted in a recording of the single "<a href="/wiki/Arnold_Layne" title="Arnold Layne">Arnold Layne</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Irgh132_55-3"><a href="#cite_note-Irgh132-55">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning17_66-3"><a href="#cite_note-manning17-66">[60]</a></sup> and the recording of other songs: "<a href="/wiki/Matilda_Mother" title="Matilda Mother">Matilda Mother</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Chapter_24" title="Chapter 24">Chapter 24</a>", "Interstellar Overdrive" and "Let's Roll Another One" (which was renamed to "<a href="/wiki/Candy_and_a_Currant_Bun" title="Candy and a Currant Bun">Candy and a Currant Bun</a>", at the suggestion of Waters). Referring to the choice of "Arnold Layne", <a href="/wiki/Nick_Mason" title="Nick Mason">Nick Mason</a> said: "We knew we wanted to be rock'n'roll stars and we wanted to make singles, so it seemed the most suitable song to condense into 3 minutes without losing too much".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manning20_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-manning20-71">[65]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-116"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-116">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Two of a Kind" was credited to <a href="/wiki/Richard_Wright_(musician)" title="Richard Wright (musician)">Richard Wright</a> on the original Peel Session release, but to Barrett on later releases, including <i><a href="/wiki/The_Best_of_Syd_Barrett:_Wouldn%27t_You_Miss_Me%3F" title="The Best of Syd Barrett: Wouldn't You Miss Me?">The Best of Syd Barrett: Wouldn't You Miss Me?</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[107]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/David_Gilmour" title="David Gilmour">David Gilmour</a>, Wright wrote the song but an increasingly confused Barrett insisted it was his own composition (and wanted to include it on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Madcap_Laughs" title="The Madcap Laughs">The Madcap Laughs</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CrazyDiamond_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-CrazyDiamond-115">[108]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-117"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-117">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">These five songs were originally released on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Peel_Session_(Syd_Barrett_album)" title="The Peel Session (Syd Barrett album)">Syd Barrett: The Peel Session</a></i>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-120"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-120">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">These three songs, along with the five from the <i>Top Gear</i> performance, were released on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Radio_One_Sessions_(Syd_Barrett_album)" title="The Radio One Sessions (Syd Barrett album)">Syd Barrett: The Radio One Sessions</a></i>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-194"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-194">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Television Personalities became the subject of controversy and derision when, as they had been selected as the opening act on Gilmour's <i><a href="/wiki/About_Face_(album)" title="About Face (album)">About Face</a></i> tour in the early 1980s, lead singer <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dan_Treacy" title="Dan Treacy">Dan Treacy</a> decided to read aloud Barrett's real home address to the audience of thousands. Gilmour removed them from the tour immediately afterwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[183]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>
